        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.3c06868"><meta property="og:title" content="SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/acsami.3c06868.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Binder-free electrodes offer a great opportunity for developing high-performance sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) aiming at the application in energy storage devices. Tin selenide (SnSe) is considered to be a promising anode material for SIBs owing to its high theoretical capacity (780 mA h g–1). In this work, a SnSe nanosheet array (SnSe NS) on a carbon cloth is prepared using a vacuum thermal evaporation method. The as-prepared SnSe NS electrode does not have metal current collectors, binders, or any conductive additives. In comparison with the electrode of SnSe blocky particles (SnSe BP), the SnSe NS electrode delivers a higher initial charge capacity of 713 mA h g–1 at a current density of 0.1C and maintains a higher charge capacity of 410 mA h g–1 after 50 cycles. Furthermore, the electrochemical behaviors of the SnSe NS electrode are determined via pseudocapacitance and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurements, indicating a faster kinetic process of the SnSe NS electrode compared to that of the SnSe BP. Operando  X-ray diffraction measurements prove that the SnSe NS exhibits better phase reversibility than the SnSe BP. After the cycles, the SnSe NS electrode still maintains its particular structure. This work provides a feasible method to prepare SnSe nanostructures with high capacity and improved sodium ion diffusion ability."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/acsami.3c06868.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931882eebe1954b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931882eebe1954b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '826fac53-a825-454a-83ae-6007f5f1a3cd'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://script.hotjar.com/modules.f98993dba31a932bc912.js"></script><script id="yui__dyn_0" type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931882eebe1954b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

		<a href="https://www.cas.org">CAS</a>
	</li>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="931882eebe1954b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "SnSe Nanosheet Array on Carbon Cloth as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c06868", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">8</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Binder-free electrodes offer a great opportunity for developing high-performance sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) aiming at the application in energy storage devices. Tin selenide (SnSe) is considered to be a promising anode material for SIBs owing to its high theoretical capacity (780 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). In this work, a SnSe nanosheet array (SnSe NS) on a carbon cloth is prepared using a vacuum thermal evaporation method. The as-prepared SnSe NS electrode does not have metal current collectors, binders, or any conductive additives. In comparison with the electrode of SnSe blocky particles (SnSe BP), the SnSe NS electrode delivers a higher initial charge capacity of 713 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1C and maintains a higher charge capacity of 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles. Furthermore, the electrochemical behaviors of the SnSe NS electrode are determined via pseudocapacitance and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurements, indicating a faster kinetic process of the SnSe NS electrode compared to that of the SnSe BP. <i>Operando</i>  X-ray diffraction measurements prove that the SnSe NS exhibits better phase reversibility than the SnSe BP. After the cycles, the SnSe NS electrode still maintains its particular structure. This work provides a feasible method to prepare SnSe nanostructures with high capacity and improved sodium ion diffusion ability.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D42811%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWenlong%2BYang%252C%2BYuncai%2BChen%252C%2BXingxing%2BYin%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D36%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c06868%26title%3DSnSe%2BNanosheet%2BArray%2Bon%2BCarbon%2BCloth%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D42822%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291484&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffraction"> Diffraction </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion"> Diffusion </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Binder-free&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_binder-free">binder-free</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Snse+Nanosheet+Array&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SnSe%20nanosheet%20array">SnSe nanosheet array</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Vacuum+Thermal+Evaporation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_vacuum%20thermal%20evaporation">vacuum thermal evaporation</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Currently, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are one of the most important types of energy storage systems. Owing to the limited resources of lithium reserves, seeking alternatives to LIBs is of utmost necessity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) that have similar chemical properties as LIBs have been considered as a promising candidate owing to the cost-effective, higher earth abundance, and wider resource distribution of sodium compared with those of lithium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 3–9">(3–9)</a> However, owing to the larger radius of sodium ions (1.02 Å) compared to that of lithium ions (0.76 Å), SIBs exhibit poor sodium diffusion kinetics and deteriorated cycling performance due to complicated (de)sodiation behaviors, limiting their practical applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> In recent decades, the development of cathode materials for SIBs has achieved great success. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10–12">(10–12)</a> The investigations on anode materials still need more attention as several challenges exit in the practical application of anode materials in SIBs, such as safety hazards from Na dendrites, low Coulombic efficiencies, poor rate capabilities, and deteriorated cycling performances. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13–16">(13–16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Carbonaceous materials, alloy materials, metal oxides, and organic materials are commonly used as the anode materials for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 17–21">(17–21)</a> Among them, carbonaceous materials are some of the most widely studied anode materials for SIBs. Hard carbon shows good sodium storage properties of low charge/discharge potential, high capacity, and good cycling stability, but it exhibits insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which severely limits its practical use. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Alloying anodes, such as tin, phosphorus, and antimony, can be alloyed with Na to produce Na-metal alloys, which show high specific capacities and low redox potentials. However, these materials would undergo severe capacity reduction upon cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> Metal oxides are expected to undergo multielectron transfer reactions during the discharge and charge processes, delivering higher reversible capacities. However, they would experience considerable volume changes during the insertion and extraction of sodium ions, and the volume change leads to rapid agglomeration and pulverization of metal oxide particles, causing rapid capacity loss. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Two-dimensional (2D) materials, such as layered metal sulfides and graphene, represent a new class of materials with unique properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> They have large specific surface areas, which can enlarge the contact area between the electrode material and the electrolyte, shorten the diffusion distance of sodium ions, and provide numerous active sites for sodium storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> Among various 2D materials, layered metal sulfides are regarded as promising anode materials for SIBs, attracting widespread attention due to high capacity, superior conductivity, and abundance of elements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> SnSe is of particular interest due to its narrow band gap and high theoretical capacity (780 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26–28">(26–28)</a> However, SnSe encounters significant challenges, such as large volume changes upon cycling, poor electronic conductivity, and slow transport kinetics. These problems can result in structural collapse and poor electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 28–31">(28–31)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Morphology control is an effective strategy to solve these problems. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 28, 29 and 32–35">(28,29,32–35)</a> For example, Liang et al. prepared a hierarchical porous pomegranate-like SnSe/ZnSe@C composite, exhibiting a specific capacity of 508.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> and maintaining a reversible capacity of 195.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 10,000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Zhao et al. prepared monodisperse SnSe nanoparticles with a unique yolk–shell structure, which had an initial charge capacity of 355.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and delivered high capacity retention of 72.5% after 150 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Yang et al. prepared SnSe nanosheets anchored on Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>, showing a three-dimensional (3D) network structure, which maintained a high capacity (240.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) after 1350 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Zhao and co-workers prepared SnSe quantum dots, delivering an initial charge capacity of 338.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and maintaining a reversible capacity of 268 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 750 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">The above-mentioned SIB anodes were primarily prepared using the traditional slurry-coating technique, in which binders and conductive carbon additives are necessary for the electrode structure. On the one hand, the use of binders, polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), carboxymethylcellulose sodium (CMC), or polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) would considerably reduce the conductivity of the electrode, thus impeding their potential application in high-performance energy storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> On the other hand, the inactive binders and conductive carbon additives would lower the gravimetric energy density of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Herein, a SnSe nanosheet array (SnSe NS) was prepared evenly on a flexible carbon cloth (CC) by vacuum thermal evaporation method, and its structure, morphology, and electrochemical performance were characterized. As an anode for SIBs, the SnSe NS electrode shows a high initial charge capacity of 713 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C and maintains 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles. Electrochemical measurements demonstrate the faster kinetic process of the SnSe NS electrode compared to that of the SnSe blocky particles (SnSe BP). Furthermore, the SnSe NS electrode can still maintain its structure after 50 cycles. Herein, the importance of nanostructure fabrication in the design of high-energy SIBs is shown.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of Electrodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">SnSe NS and BP electrodes were synthesized using a vacuum thermal evaporation method on the substrate of a flexible CC, which is similar to the method given in a previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Before the experiment, the CC was ultrasonically cleaned for 15 min with acetone, ethanol, and deionized water successively and dried at 60 °C for 12 h. SnSe powder was purchased from Alfa Aesar (99.999%), which was used as the evaporation source. The CC in the shape of a disk with a 1 cm<sup>2</sup> size was used as the substrate. The evaporation was heated to 450 °C and kept for 90 min after the chamber was evacuated to a vacuum of ≈1 × 10<sup>–4</sup> Pa. To adjust the microstructures of NS and BP, the substrate temperatures were set to room temperature (25 °C) and 200 °C, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure of the sample was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) using a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation in the 2θ range of 20–60°. The morphology of the sample was acquired using a Hitachi SU8010 field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) and a FEI Talos F200S transmission electron microscope (TEM) equipped with an energy dispersive X-ray spectrometer (EDS). The valence state of the element was investigated using a Thermo Scientific Escalab 250 Xi X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS). The specific surface area was calculated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method from N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms using a Micromeritics ASAP 2460 analyzer.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">SnSe NS and BP electrodes were used as anodes directly, and the loading of SnSe on the CC was ≈1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The electrochemical performance of the electrode was measured using 2032-type coin cells. Sodium metal was used as the counter electrode. The electrolyte was prepared by dissolving 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) at a weight ratio of 1:1 with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements were performed on a Neware battery testing system. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted on a Solartron 1470E electrochemical workstation. The frequency range of the EIS was 0.01 Hz–100 kHz. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements were carried out on the Neware battery testing system at 78 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (0.1C) with a 30 min interval followed by an open circuit rest of 20 min. The diffusion coefficient of sodium ion was calculated according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" vertical-align: 0em; border-top: 1.3px solid; width: 2.673em; height: 0px;"></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 1.082em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-40" style="vertical-align: -0.452em;"><span><span style="font-size: 111%; font-family: STIXSizeTwoSym;">)</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -5.054em; left: 4.207em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-41" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-42"><span class="msup" id="MathJax-Span-43"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.923em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(2.446em, 1003.36em, 5.003em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-44"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-45" style="vertical-align: -0.452em;"><span><span style="font-size: 111%; font-family: STIXSizeTwoSym;">(</span></span></span><span class="mfrac" id="MathJax-Span-46"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.935em; height: 0px; margin-right: 0.116em; margin-left: mathvariant="normal">a</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mn>4</mn><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><mrow><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi>m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></msub><msub><mi>V</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi></msub></mrow><mrow><msub><mi>M</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></msub><mi>S</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup></mrow><mrow><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi pulse, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the mass of the host material, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is the molar volume of the electrode, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> is the molecular weight, <i>S</i> is the surface area of the electrode, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the change in the potential (<i>V</i>) at a plateau, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> is the change in the potential (<i>V</i>) during the pulse time.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.4.  <i>Operando</i> XRD</h3><div class="NLM_p last"><i>Operando</i> XRD analysis was performed using an in-house designed cell. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> A thin beryllium plate, which is transparent for X-rays, was utilized as a current collector for the electrode material. The electrode slurry composed of SnSe, super P, and poly(vinylidene difluoride) (PVdF) at a weight ratio of 8:1:1 in an appropriate amount of 1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP) was directly cast onto the beryllium plate, and the coated plate was dried at 60 °C overnight. A sodium metal plate was used as the counter electrode, while the electrolyte (1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC and DMC at a weight ratio of 1:1 with 5 wt % FEC) was used to wet the Whatman glass fiber separator. The cell was discharged/charged at 78 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (0.1C) in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. At the same time, XRD was performed in the 2θ range of 25–55°.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a shows the synthesis of SnSe NS and BP electrodes using a vacuum thermal evaporation method. Compared with traditional electrodes used in batteries, the as-prepared SnSe electrode constitutes a completely binder-free and flexible electrode. It can be easily bent without any damage, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,c. To investigate the crystal structure and morphology of the electrodes, XRD and FESEM were employed to characterize the samples. The XRD patterns of the as-prepared SnSe NS and BP electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d,e, respectively. Notice that the reflections of both electrodes match with the standard card of SnSe (SnSe JCPDS#48-1224) in addition to the reflection of the CC (a broad peak between 22 and 27°). The orientations of the two samples are different, which can be attributed to the different thermodynamic conditions. The preferential crystal surface of the SnSe is the (100) plane due to its lowest surface energy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> As the substrate temperature increases, the orientation of the (400) plane will also increase. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f,g, the SnSe NS electrode shows the morphology of a nanosheet array, and the SnSe BP electrode has the distinct morphology of blocky particles. The lateral size and thickness of the SnSe NS are 1–5 μm and ≈10 nm, respectively, and the particle size of the SnSe BP ranges from 100 to 500 nm. The EDS elemental mapping results prove that both Sn and Se elements are uniformly distributed on the CC. The microstructure of nanosheet array usually has a large specific surface area, which is beneficial for increasing the electrode and the electrolyte contact area and shortening the diffusion pathway of sodium ions.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of SnSe NS and BP electrodes. (b) Front view and (c) side view of flexible and binder-free SnSe electrodes. XRD patterns of SnSe: (d) NS and (e) BP. SEM images and corresponding EDS elemental mappings of SnSe (f) NS and (g) BP in the selected areas.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To determine the chemical states of the SnSe NS and BP, the samples were further characterized by XPS. The corresponding XPS spectra are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. The Sn 3d spectra of the SnSe NS and BP, shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a,b</a>, respectively, display two obvious peaks with binding energies of 486.7 and 495.3 eV, indicating that the valence state of Sn is 2+ in the samples. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 35, 41 and 42">(35,41,42)</a> The other two small peaks with binding energies of 485.3 and 493.7 eV correspond to the Sn–C bond, indicating that the SnSe NS and BP are tightly combined with CC, and the composite electrodes exhibit electronic coupling between them. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The Se 3d spectra of the SnSe NS and BP are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c,d</a>, respectively, in which two peaks with binding energies of 53.6 and 54.5 eV corresponding to Se<sup>2–</sup> match well with the previous reports of SnSe materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">TEM was used to further study the microstructure and elemental composition of SnSe NS and BP. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a shows the TEM image of the SnSe NS, in which the 2D structure is clearly observed. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in the selected area of the SnSe NS is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, in which two lattice fringes with an interlayer spacing of 3.0 Å correspond to (011) and (0–11) planes of SnSe. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of the SnSe NS in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c represents the spot pattern of SnSe along the [100]-zone axis. As shown in the EDS results of the SnSe NS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2a</a>), the elements are distributed uniformly, and the atomic ratio of Sn/Se is close to 1. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e shows the TEM image of the SnSe BP, and the corresponding HRTEM image is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f. Clear lattice fringes of 3.0 and 3.3 Å corresponding to the (011) and (210) planes of SnSe, respectively, are observed. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g displays the SAED pattern of the SnSe BP, corresponding to the pattern of SnSe along the [1–22]-zone axis. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2b</a>, the Sn and Se elements distribute homogeneously, and the ratio of Sn/Se is close to 1 in the SnSe BP.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) TEM image, (b) HRTEM image, and (c) SAED pattern of SnSe NS. (d) Corresponding elemental distribution in SnSe NS. (e) TEM image, (f) HRTEM image, and (g) SAED pattern of SnSe BP. (h) Corresponding elemental distribution in SnSe BP.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Based on the aforementioned results, SnSe materials with different morphologies can be facilely obtained using the vacuum thermal evaporation method by adjusting the substrate temperature. The SnSe NS and BP on the CC would be of interest when used as anodes for SIBs, as no conductive carbon additives and binders are needed in the electrodes. In the following, the electrochemical performance of SnSe NS and BP is introduced. The capacity retentions of the SnSe NS and BP are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. The capacity contribution of the CC has been deducted, and the cycling and rate capacities of the blank CC are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a,b</a>, respectively. As evident, the initial charge capacity and discharge capacity of the SnSe NS are 713 and 769 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which are higher than those of the SnSe BP (570 and 674 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). Furthermore, the SnSe NS delivers a higher ICE of 92.7% than the SnSe BP (84.5%), and the irreversible capacity loss is mainly caused by the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> It is worth noting that the SnSe NS exhibits a higher ICE owing to the design of the nanostructure. When the electrolyte encounters the nanosheet array structure that is binder-free, the SEI film forms directly on the entire surface of the individual ordered nanosheet during the initial cycle. This effectively prevents further consumption of the electrolyte, ensuring not only a high ICE but also promoting the achievement of stable cycling efficiency and cycling behavior. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Additionally, the SnSe material participates in the conversion reaction during the cycling process, and the nanosheet array structure not only effectively reduces the volume expansion but also shortens the diffusion distance of the sodium ions. As a result, their reaction kinetics is improved and the stability of reversible transformation is enhanced, which also facilitates the generation of a higher ICE. Similar methods of enhancing ICE and electrochemical performance by the design of nanostructures or morphological regulation have been reported in previous studies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 46–49">(46–49)</a> After the initial cycle, the Coulombic efficiency of the SnSe NS electrode increases remarkably and then reaches almost 100%, indicating its excellent reversibility. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a> shows the comparison of the SnSe NS with other SnSe-based anodes reported in the literature, indicating that the novel structure of the SnSe NS has the advantage of large capacity for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 28, 29 and 31–35">(28,29,31–35)</a> After 50 cycles at 0.1C for the SnSe NS and BP, the capacity of the SnSe NS is retained at 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 57% of its initial capacity, evidently higher than that of the SnSe BP (13 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 2.2% of the initial capacity). The decrease in capacity with an increasing number of cycles for the SnSe BP may be attributed to the significant volume changes of active materials, leading to irreversible structural changes and degradation of electrochemical activity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Furthermore, the rate capabilities of SnSe NS and BP at rates from 0.1 to 2.0C and then back to 0.1C are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. The SnSe NS demonstrates higher capacities at each rate than the SnSe BP. When the rate is set back to 0.1C, the SnSe NS can recover 85% of the initial capacity, whereas the SnSe BP can only recover 76% of the initial capacity, demonstrating that the SnSe NS exhibits better rate capability than the SnSe BP. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d shows the corresponding discharge–charge curves of the SnSe NS and BP electrodes at different rates, respectively. Notice that the SnSe NS delivers larger capacities and smaller polarization than the SnSe BP at each rate. Additionally, the long-cycle stability of the SnSe NS electrode is determined at a high current density of 1.0C, and the SnSe NS electrode retains a capacity of 161 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 250 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Cycling performances of SnSe NS and BP at 0.1C. (b) Rate capabilities of SnSe NS and BP at rates ranging from 0.1 to 2.0C and then back to 0.1C. Discharge/charge curves of SnSe (c) NS and (d) BP at different rates. (e) Cycling performance of SnSe NS at 1.0C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Comparison of the Capacity and ICE of SnSe NS with Those of Other Reported SnSe-Based Anodes for SIBs</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table
