char=".">63.0</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–</sup><sup>1</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnSe@Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">electrostatic self-assembly and annealing</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">3D network</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">208.4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">40.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–</sup><sup>1</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnSe</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">electrospinning and high energy ball milling</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">quantum dots</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">338.7</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">72.6</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–</sup><sup>1</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnSe/SnTe@N-CNFs</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">electrospinning and calcination</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">heterostructure nanoparticles</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼496</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">80.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–</sup><sup>1</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnSe@NSC</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">one-step carbonization and selenization method</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">core/shell hybrid spheres</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">604.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">89.9</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–</sup><sup>1</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">SnSe NS</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">vacuum thermal evaporation</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">nanosheet array</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">713</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">92.7</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">78&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–</sup><sup>1</sup></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">this work</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the effect of the substrate on the structure, morphology, and electrochemical performance of SnSe, we used nickel foil to replace the CC, while keeping other experimental parameters the same. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>, the nanosheet array morphology mainly depends on the substrate temperature. The XRD pattern of the SnSe NS on the nickel foil verifies that the same phase of SnSe as the SnSe NS on the CC is obtained except for the substrate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>). Although the substrate would not change the structure and morphology of SnSe, the capacity and cycling performance of the SnSe NS on the nickel foil are much lower than those of the SeSe NS on the CC, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c</a>. This phenomenon may be ascribed to the smooth surface of the nickel foil, resulting in the exfoliation of the SnSe NS from the nickel foil during discharge/charge processes. In comparison with the pristine SnSe powder, the SnSe NS on the CC also delivers better cycling performance, proving the advantage of this electrode structure. In addition, the SnSe NS electrode exhibits a stable capacity of 285 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2C even after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4d</a>). The Coulombic efficiency during cycling approaches 100%, indicating its excellent reversibility. Moreover, as the composite electrode, the SnSe nanosheet array and carbon cloth (NS/CC) exhibit a higher capacity of 131 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles at 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> than the SnSe blocky particles and carbon cloth (BP/CC), which has a capacity of only 81 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>). Even at higher rates from 160 to 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, SnSe NS/CC always delivers higher capacities than SnSe BP/CC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>). According to the BET method, the specific surface area of the SnSe NS is 6.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, which is larger than that of the SnSe BP (2.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), and the pore size distribution curve indicates that the SnSe NS has a relatively narrow pore size distribution of ≈12 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The satisfactory electrochemical performance of the SnSe NS can be ascribed to its large specific area and void space, enabling direct contact with the electrolyte and shortening the sodium ion diffusion distance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To realize the superior electrochemical performance of the SnSe NS compared to the SnSe BP, <i>operando</i> XRD and CV measurements were performed to investigate the phase variation and reaction mechanisms upon (de)sodiation. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b shows the <i>operando</i>  XRD results of the SnSe NS electrode. It is evident that the intensities of Be (45.8, 50.9, and 52.8°; JCPDS no. 22-0111) and BeO (38.8, 41.5 and 44.2°; JCPDS no. 78-1559) remain constant during the electrochemical stage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> The diffraction peaks at 29.4, 30.5, 31.1, 37.8, 43.3, and 49.7° correspond to the crystallographic planes (011), (111), (400), (311), (411), and (511) of SnSe, respectively. Upon sodiation, the diffraction reflections of SnSe decrease, and two weaker broad peaks appear at 34.8 and 37.2°, which are indexed to the (510) plane of Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn and (220) plane of Na<sub>2</sub>Se, respectively, verifying the phase evolution. The phases of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn verify that the conversion reactions of SnSe + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup> → Sn + Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Sn + 3.75Na<sup>+</sup> + 3.75e<sup>–</sup> → Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn occur during cycling, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> In the subsequent charging cycle, the signals at 29.4, 30.5, and 31.1° specifically regenerate, indicating the reversibility of the SnSe NS upon cycling. In addition, other diffractions of the SnSe NS do not exhibit enhancement during the charging process, which is possibly due to the reduced crystallinity and diminutive size of intermediates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Comparatively, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> shows the <i>operando</i>  XRD results of the SnSe BP electrode. Notice that the crystal planes involved in phase evolution are not identical to those of the SnSe NS, and the degree of the phase variation is not obvious as in the SnSe NS. The observed peaks for the phase evolution of the SnSe BP do not show distinct regeneration, indicating indistinct reversibility during the cycling process. Additionally, the signals corresponding to Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn and Na<sub>2</sub>Se are comparatively weaker than those of the SnSe NS, suggesting a poor conversion reaction mechanism.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) <i>Operando</i>  XRD patterns together with initial discharge/charge curves and (b) 2D contour image of the SnSe NS electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b, SnSe NS and BP show similar redox peaks. The two strong peaks at approximately 0 and 0.25 V can be ascribed to the redox reaction of the CC, which can be verified by its CV curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). In the cathodic scan, the broad peak at 1.1 V is assigned to Na<sub>2</sub>Se and Sn formed in the conversion reaction and the generation of SEI. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The reductive peak at ≈1.0 V also represents the conversion reaction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref50" aria-label="reference 31 and 50">(31,50)</a> and the reductive peak at ≈0.7 V is ascribed to the Na–Sn alloying process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The reduction peak at 1.06 V is attributed to the conversion of Sn to SnSe, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> and the weak peak at 1.58 V is due to the deintercalation of Na ions from the interlayer of SnSe. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> The area of CV curves reflects the capacity of the electrode, and the current value indicates the rate of the reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The SnSe NS displays CV curves with larger areas and peaks with higher currents compared to the SnSe BP, revealing a higher capacity and better kinetics of the SnSe NS. In addition, the SnSe NS displays CV curves that are more stable than the SnSe BP during cycling, indicating better cycling performance of the SnSe NS.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. CV curves of SnSe (a) NS and (b) BP at 0.05 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. (c) Nyquist plots of SnSe NS and BP electrodes. (d) Distribution of relaxation time results of SnSe NS and BP from Nyquist plots. (e) GITT plots of SnSe NS and corresponding variations in the sodium ion diffusion coefficient. (f) GITT plots of SnSe BP and corresponding variations in the sodium ion diffusion coefficient.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Meanwhile, EIS measurements were carried out before cycling for the SnSe NS and BP electrodes to study sodium-ion diffusion kinetics. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c, both Nyquist plots show a depressed semicircle in the high-frequency region and a sloped straight line in the low-frequency region. The former represents the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) between the electrode and electrolyte, and the latter corresponds to the sodium-ion diffusion ability of the electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> It can be seen from the Nyquist plots that the diameter of the semicircle for the SnSe NS electrode is smaller than that for the SnSe BP electrode, and the SnSe NS electrode displays a larger slope of the straight line than the SnSe BP electrode, indicating the faster ion diffusion kinetics between the interface and the bulk material for the SnSe NS, leading to an increase in the electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> To further investigate the electrochemical process in the electrode, the distribution of relaxation time was carried out to distinguish the different electrochemical processes with different time constants. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d, the time constant (τ) is characteristic of each polarization process and the area under the peaks reflects the contribution of the polarization resistance of a specific reaction to the total polarization of the cell. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> According to previous reports, the peaks can be separated into three main electrochemical processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 58 and 59">(58,59)</a> The P1 peaks are attributed to the interfacial resistance; the P2 peak corresponds to the charge transfer process; and the P3 peak represents the diffusion process. Evidently, the diffusion process is dominant in the whole electrochemical process, and the SnSe NS displays a lower P3 peak than the SnSe BP, proving its higher sodium-ion diffusion kinetics. GITT measurements were performed to calculate the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e,f, the average <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of the SnSe NS electrode is calculated to be ≈4.8 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, whereas that of the SnSe BP electrode is calculated to be ≈3.0 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. The higher <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of the SnSe NS contributes to its better rate capability compared to that of the SnSe BP.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further study the kinetics of the SnSe NS and BP electrodes, CV measurements were conducted at various scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a,b, the currents of the anodic and cathodic peaks increase with the scan rates. Meanwhile, the CV curves are broadened and shift from the initial position due to the increased polarization and ohmic resistance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The SnSe NS generates higher peak currents than the SnSe BP at the same scan rate, indicating that the SnSe NS has a higher sodium-ion storage capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> The pseudocapacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution of the whole capacity can be analyzed based on the following relationship of peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>), as shown by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0" data-mathml="<math xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML&quot; The <i>b</i> value of 0.5 indicates the diffusion-controlled behavior, whereas the <i>b</i> value of 1.0 indicates the pseudocapacitive process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61 ref63" aria-label="reference 61 and 63">(61,63)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c,d, the <i>b</i> values of the SnSe NS electrode are calculated to be 0.60 for the reduction peak and 0.69 for the oxidation peak. By contrast, the <i>b</i> values of the SnSe BP electrode are calculated as 0.57 for the reduction peak and 0.64 for the oxidation peak. Therefore, both the SnSe NS and BP exhibit diffusion-controlled behavior and pseudocapacitive behavior. Furthermore, the SnSe NS electrode has <i>b</i> values higher than those of the SnSe BP electrode, indicating the more pseudocapacitive behavior of the SnSe NS electrode.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/medium/am3c06868_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. CV curves of SnSe (a) NS and (b) BP electrodes at different scan rates. Fitting plots of SnSe (c) NS and (d) BP between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i>. Diffusion-controlled and pseudocapacitive contributions of SnSe (e) NS and (f) BP. Pseudocapacitive contributions of SnSe (g) NS and (h) BP at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/asset/images/large/am3c06868_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c06868&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The pseudocapacitive contribution can be calculated by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><div class="MathJax_Display" style="text-align: left;"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0" data-mathml="<math xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML&quot; display=&quot;block&quot; indentalign=&quot;left&quot;><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift=&quot;3em&quot; indentshiftlast=&quot;3em&quot;><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak=&quot;nobreak&quot;>=</mo><mstyle indentshift=&quot;3em&quot; indentshiftlast=&quot;3em&quot;><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><msup><mi>v</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math>" role="presentation" style="text-align: left; position: relative;"><nobr aria-hidden="true"><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-74" style="width: 7.162em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 6.48em; height: 0px; font-size: 110%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.253em, 1006.48em, 2.673em, -999.997em); top: -2.327em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-75"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-76"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-77"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-78"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-79"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-80" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑖</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-81" style="padding-left: 0.344em;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-82" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">=</span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-83" style="padding-left: 0.344em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-84"><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-85"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.969em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1000.51em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-86" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑘</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.571em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-87" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">1</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-88" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑣</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-89" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular; padding-left: 0.23em;">+</span><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-90" style="padding-left: 0.23em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.969em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.128em, 1000.51em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-91" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑘</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.571em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-92" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="msup" id="MathJax-Span-93"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.48em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.355em, 1000.46em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-94" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝑣</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.372em; left: 0.514em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-95"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-96" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">1</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-97" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">/</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-98" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.332em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.253em;"></span></span></nobr><span class="MJX_Assistive_MathML MJX_Assistive_MathML_Block" role="presentation"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><msup><mi>v</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></span></span></div><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><msup><mi>v</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the pseudocapacitive and ion diffusion contributions, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e,f, the pseudocapacitive contributions of the SnSe NS are 28, 51, 66, and 86% at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1.0 mV s<sup>–1,</sup> respectively, which are higher than those of the SnSe BP (24, 36, 51, and 73%). The SnSe NS has a higher pseudocapacitive contribution in the kinetic process than the SnSe BP, which would facilitate its ion transfer during the reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> Therefore, it can be one of the reasons that leads to the better electrochemical performance of the SnSe NS. The pseudocapacitive contributions to the total current of the SnSe NS and BP at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g,h in detail, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p last">To investigate the microstructure changes in the SnSe NS and BP after 50 cycles, the cycled cells were disassembled, and the harvest electrodes were characterized by XRD and SEM. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a,b</a>, the SnSe NS electrode still exhibits obvious reflections of SnSe after 50 cycles, but no obvious reflections of SnSe could be observed in the cycled SnSe BP. The results indicate a comparative structural stability to a degree for the SnSe NS electrode. In addition, the phase of N<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> may be derived from the discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> From the SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c–f</a>), the SnSe NS electrode maintains a nanosheet morphology after cycling, whereas the SnSe BP electrode changes to large lumps with obvious cracks, indicating that the SnSe NS electrode could adapt better to the volume changes upon cycling. Furthermore, the elemental distribution of Sn and Se is still uniformly distributed on the CC for the SnSe NS after cycling, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. In contrast, the SnSe BP electrode shows a distinct surface morphology after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9d,f</a>) from its pristine state (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g). The surface is possibly covered by a thick SEI film and obvious cracks are observed, which results in the reduction of sodium-ion diffusion between the particles and a low capacity.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the SnSe NS was synthesized by a vacuum thermal evaporation method on a flexible CC as an SIB anode without any binders or conductive carbon additives. Benefiting from the NS structure, the SnSn NS electrode showed superior electrochemical performance compared to the SnSe BP electrode. In particular, the initial charge capacity of the SnSe NS reached 713 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C, and a capacity of 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was maintained after 50 cycles. This structure could effectively adapt to volume changes and prevent the pulverization of electrodes during cycling. Electrochemical kinetics results indicated that the SnSe NS exhibited more pseudocapacitive contribution and faster sodium-ion diffusion kinetics than the SnSe BP. Although the capacity retention is not as high as demonstrated in this study, which is probably due to the weak adhesion of SnSe to the carbon cloth, the SnSe nanostructure still provides considerable abilities to maximize capacities and improve sodium ion diffusion kinetics due to the advantage of the nanostructure. The drawback of capacity retention can be addressed by future nanostructure design. Therefore, this study demonstrated the importance of nanostructure fabrication in the design of high-energy-density SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c06868?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c06868</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XPS spectra of SnSe NS and BP; TEM–EDS spectra and the corresponding quantitative results of SnSe NS and SnSe BP; cycling performance and rate capability of CC; SEM, XRD, and cycling performance of SnSe NS on nickel foil, and cycling performance of pristine SnSe powder and SnSe NS on CC; cycling performance and rate capabilities of SnSe NS/CC and SnSe BP/CC as composite electrodes; nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherm curves of SnSe NS and SnSe BP; <i>operando</i> XRD results of SnSe BP electrode; CV curves of CC; XRD and SEM of SnSe NS and BP electrodes after 50 cycles; SEM together with elemental mapping results of SnSe NS electrode after 50 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c06868/suppl_file/am3c06868_si_001.pdf">am3c06868_si_001.pdf (1.41 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.3c06868" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent advances in effective protection of sodium metal anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Linlin; Li, Xifei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">53</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">630-642</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of the ultrahigh theor. capacity, sodium metal is regarded as the most prospective next-generation anode material.  However, due to the dendritic sodium formation as well as high chem. reactivity with electrolyte, it still has safety issues and poor cyclability, which have severely impeded the practicability of sodium metal technol.  Therefore, it is important for the advanced sodium metal batteries to construct the stable dendrite-free sodium metal anode.  In recent years, considerable efforts have been directed towards solving these problems, including sodium-electrode framework design, org. electrolyte modification, sodium-metal interface protection.  Furthermore, solid-state electrolytes have an advantage over the liq. electrolytes and are considered to constitute safe solid sodium metal batteries so as to prohibit problematic sodium dendrites.  This review comprehensively analyzes the growth mechanism of the sodium dendrites and summarizes recent advances in the strategies of suppressing the formation of sodium dendrite.  The practical application in full batteries and future challenges of sodium metal anode are addressed for energy storage devices.  We hope this review delivers an overall picture of sodium protection strategies and inspires more researches in the future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1126/science.aay9972"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, C.</span></span>;                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational design of layered oxide materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Chenglong; Wang, Qidi; Yao, Zhenpeng; Wang, Jianlin; Sanchez-Lengeling, Benjamin; Ding, Feixiang; Qi, Xingguo; Lu, Yaxiang; Bai, Xuedong; Li, Baohua; Li, Hong; Aspuru-Guzik, Alan; Huang, Xuejie; Delmas, Claude; Wagemaker, Marnix; Chen, Liquan; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

The performance of such batteries is limited by available electrode materials, esp. for sodium-ion layered oxides, motivating the exploration of high compositional diversity.  How the compn. dets. the structural chem. is decisive for the electrochem. performance but very challenging to predict, esp. for complex compns.  The "cationic potential" is introduced that captures the key interactions of layered materials and makes it possible to predict the stacking structures.  This is demonstrated through the rational design and prepn. of layered electrode materials with improved performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-based batteries have attracted wide interests in the academic and industrial fields.  However, their energy d. is still lower than that of Li-based batteries.  Here we report an initial anode-free Na battery with an energy d. of over 200 Wh kg-1, which is even higher than that of the com. LiFePO4||graphite battery.  Through introducing graphitic carbon coating on the Al current collector and boron-contg. electrolytes in the battery, we show that uniform nucleation and robust interphases enable reversible and crack-free Na deposition.  Benefitting from the synergetic effects derived from the built cooperative interfaces, the cycling lifetime of the Na battery without applying addnl. pressure reaches 260 cycles, which is the longest life for large-size cells with zero excess Na.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ionic liquid-assisted solvothermal synthesis of hollow Mn2O3 anode and LiMn2O4 cathode materials for Li-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Xin; Wang, Jun; Jia, Haiping; Kloepsch, Richard; Liu, Haidong; Beltrop, Kolja; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Mn-based Mn2O3 anode and LiMn2O4 cathode materials were prepd. by a solvothermal method combined with post annealing process.  Environmentally friendly ionic liq. 1-Butyl-3-methylimidazolium tetrafluoroborate as both structure-directing agent and F source was used to prep. hollow polyhedron MnF2 precursor.  Both target materials Mn2O3 anode and LiMn2O4 cathode have the morphol. of the MnF2 precursor.  The Mn2O3 anode using CM-cellulose as binder could deliver slight better electrochem. performance than the one using poly(vinyldifluoride) as binder.  The former has an initial charge capacity of 800 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 101.8 mA g-1, and exhibits no obvious capacity decay for 150 cycles at 101.8 mA g-1.  The LiMn2O4 cathode material prepd. with molten salt assistant could display much better electrochem. performance than the one prepd. without molten salt assistance.  In particular, it has an initial discharge capacity of 117.5 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 0.5 C and good rate capability.  In the field of Li ion batteries, both the Mn2O3 anode and LiMn2O4 cathode materials could exhibit enhanced electrochem. performance due to the well formed morphol. based on the ionic liq.-assisted solvothermal method.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.05.056"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Risthaus, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Friesen, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wilken, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Berghus, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of Spinel LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with Secondary Plate Morphology as Cathode Material for Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of spinel LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 with secondary plate morphology as cathode material for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Risthaus, Tim; Wang, Jun; Friesen, Alex; Wilken, Andrea; Berghus, Debbie; Winter, Martin; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">293</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">137-142</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 material has been synthesized by a spray drying process and subsequent solid state reaction.  Polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) is given as additive to the spray drying precursor soln. and its effects on structural and electrochem. properties are evaluated.  By using PVP in the synthesis process, the obtained sample displays a secondary plate morphol. which is consisting of densely arranged primary octahedrally shaped particles.  The new cathode material has a lesser degree of impurity phases, a higher discharge capacity, a superior rate capability, and a slightly better cycling performance than the sample synthesized without PVP.  In more detail, by the use of PVP the ratio of Mn3+ to Mn4+ in the final product decreases from 20.8 to 9.2%.  The initial discharge capacity at 0.1 C exhibits an increase of about 14%.  The normalized capacity at 20 C is 84.1% instead of 67.0%.  A slightly improved cycling performance with the capacity retention increase from 93.8 to 97.9% could be obsd. as well.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b01135"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Risthaus, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stan, M.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Additive-Free Self-Presodiation Strategy for High-Performance Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Feixiang; Meng, Qingshi; Yu, Pengfei; Wang, Haibo; Niu, Yaoshen; Li, Yuqi; Yang, Yang; Rong, Xiaohui; Liu, Xiaosong; Lu, Yaxiang; Chen, Liquan; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The irreversible consumption of sodium at the anode side during the first cycle prominently reduces the energy d. of Na-ion batteries.  Different sacrificial cathode additives have been recently reported to address this problem; however, crit. issues such as byproducts (e.g., CO2) release during cycling and incompatibility with current battery fabrication procedures potentially deteriorate the full-cell performance and prevent the practical application.  Herein, an additive-free self-presodiation strategy is proposed to create lattice-coherent but component-dependent O3-NaxTMMnO2 (TM = transition metal ion(s)) cathodes by a quenching treatment rather than the general natural cooling. This study presents the route to rational design cathode materials with sodium reservoir property to simplify the presodiation process as well as improve the full-cell performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.1c02107"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Mn-Rich Phosphate Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries with Superior Rate Performance</span>. <i>ACS Energy Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">97</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">107</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

After further in-depth exploration and investigation, we believe that Na-ion batteries are promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for low cost and large-scale energy storage systems in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1016/j.rser.2019.109549"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Perveen, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Siddiq, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shahzad, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ihsan, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ahmad, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shahzad, M.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Prospects in anode materials for sodium ion batteries - A review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Perveen, Tahira; Siddiq, Muhammad; Shahzad, Nadia; Ihsan, Rida; Ahmad, Abrar; Shahzad, Muhammad Imran</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Renewable &amp; Sustainable Energy Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">109549</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSERFH</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the rapid expansion in energy demands and depletion of fossil fuel reservoirs, the importance of clean energy prodn. and storage has increased drastically.  The renewable energy recourses are cost effective, sustainable and carbon dioxide emission free alternatives.  Nevertheless, this energy is not always available and needs to be stored.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are rapidly used in various applications such as powering electronics, elec. vehicles and grid energy storage.  However, the increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy and rise in cost of LIBs due to limited availability of lithium reserves arises doubts whether LIBs alone can meet the rising demands for mid-to-large-scale energy storage.  Therefore, attention has been shifted towards development of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) which have wide reserves and low precursor cost and thus is considered as appropriate choice for solar and wind energy development.  The prime problem encountered in development of large-scale SIBs is the low effectiveness of appropriate anode material because of large size and sluggish kinetics of Na ions.  A comprehensive study regarding anode materials is reported focusing on storage mechanism and structural changes involved during storage of Na ions in various classes of anode materials including carbon-based materials, conversion, conversion/alloying and org. materials.  A brief overview of various components of SIBs such as cathode, electrolyte and separator are also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.esci.2021.12.004"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Confining Ultrafine Tin Monophosphide in Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> Interlayers for Rapid and Stable Sodium Ion Storage</span>. <i>eScience</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">203</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">211</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lignin-derived nitrogen-doped porous carbon as a high-rate anode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Du, Leilei; Wu, Wei; Luo, Chao; Xu, Dongwei; Guo, Hanyu; Wang, Ruo; Zhang, Tian; Wang, Jun; Deng, Yonghong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nitrogen-doped carbon anode materials have been prepd. by a hard-template (SiO2 nanoparticles) method, in which alkali lignin-derived azo polymer (AL-azo-NO2) was used as a low-cost carbon precursor.  The as-prepd. N-doped carbon materials show a honeycomb-like morphol. with uniform nanopores.  These materials are able to reversibly insert Na+ ions and show promising cycling performance.  In particular, the porous carbon material with larger surface area and pore vol. delivers a higher initial capacity of 205 mAh g-1 in the voltage range of 0.01-2 V at 50 mA g-1, compared to the material with lower surface area and pore vol. (only 155 mAh g-1).  The former also exhibits much better rate capability than the latter.  Even at a high c.d. of 1 A g-1, it shows a high specific capacity of 101 mAh g-1 after 1100 cycles, which retains 92% of its initial capacity.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results prove that the enhanced electrochem. performance is attributed to the improved Na+-ions kinetics in this material.  This work presents an alternative approach for the synthesis of high-rate carbonaceous anode materials for sodium ion batteries from low cost and sustainable feedstock.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.105738"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boost Sodium-Ion Batteries to Commercialization: Strategies to Enhance Initial Coulombic Efficiency of Hard Carbon Anode</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>82</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">105738</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boost sodium-ion batteries to commercialization: Strategies to enhance initial Coulombic efficiency of hard carbon anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Minghao; Li, Yu; Wu, Feng; Bai, Ying; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">82</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">105738</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as one of the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage system due to low cost and inexhaustible sodium reserves.  The commercialize application of SIBs relies on the development of advanced cathode and anode materials.  Among the various available anode materials, hard carbon material is considered to be the most potential anode material, which presents appropriate sodiation/desodiation elec. potential, high capacity, and simple synthesis method.  However, the insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of hard carbon severely restricts the practical commercialization in SIBs.  Hence, we review the influence elements that cause an inadequate ICE of hard carbon, such as the decompn. of electrolytes, high defects concn., excessive surface functional groups, and irreversible sodium ions and so on.  In order to obtain a moderate ICE, strategies including structure and morphol. modification, defect and surface engineering, compn. adjustment, electrolyte and binder optimization, material pre-treatment are confirmed to be effective.  This review provides a further understanding of obtaining hard carbon electrodes with high ICE, which will contribute to the prosperity of SIBs in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201808745"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rui, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Peering into Alloy Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Current Trends, Challenges, and Opportunities</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered tin sulfides have attracted great interest as high-capacity anode materials in Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and Na-ion batteries (NIBs).  In this review, we focus on the recent research progress in the area of design and synthesis of tin sulfide and selenide (SnS, SnS2, SnSe, and SnSe2) based anode materials for LIBs and NIBs.  After a brief introduction of the energy concerns and the development prospects of LIBs and NIBs, we further detailed the properties and advantages of tin sulfide and selenide based anode materials for LIBs and NIBs.  Besides the material structure design and optimization, the underlying mechanism and theor. anal. for improved electrochem. performance are also presented.  Addnl., comparison of tin sulfides and selenides is also provided.  Innovative strategies that have demonstrated the effectiveness of enhancing the performance of tin sulfide and selenide based anode materials for LIBs and NIBs are summarized.  We hope that this timely review can shed light on the research and development of tin sulfides and selenides as high-performance anode materials which are not only a good supplement to the material pool of commercialized LIBs, but also help facilitate the development of low-cost and sustainable NIBs for stationary energy storage in the future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2015.07.140"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">SnSe/Carbon Nanocomposite Synthesized by High Energy Ball Milling as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin selenide (SnSe) is considered as a potential anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high theor. specific capacity.  Unfortunately, it suffers from drastic vol. expansion/contraction during sodium ions insertion/extn., resulting in poor cycling stability.  Herein, a pomegranate-inspired porous carbon shell wrapped heterogeneous SnSe/ZnSe composite (SnSe/ZnSe and C) is exquisitely designed and fabricated through electrostatic spraying followed by high-temp. selenization.  The polyacrylonitrile-derived carbon shell acts as an adhesive to link the porous cubic SnSe/ZnSe and form highly interconnected microcircuits to improve the electron/ion transfer efficiency and inhibit the bulk vol. change of internal metallic selenide nanoparticles and polyselenides dissoln. during repeated cycling.  Moreover, the abundant heterostructure interface of SnSe/ZnSe further significantly accelerates the electrons/ions transport.  As a result, the as-prepd. SnSe/ZnSe and C electrode exhibit a high-specific capacity (508.3 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1), excellent rate performance (177.8 mAh g-1 at 10.0 A g-1), and remarkable cycling stability (195.9 mAh g-1 after 10,000 cycles at 5.0 A g-1).  Furthermore, in-situ X-ray diffraction (XRD)/Raman, ex-situ transmission electron microscopy, and kinetic anal. clearly reveal a four-step Electrochem. reaction process and battery-capacitor dual-mode sodium storage mechanism.  This work provides a new perspective for developing com. SIBs anode materials with high capacity and long lifespan.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1039/C8QI01337C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Di, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Quan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Yolk-Shell Structured SnSe as a High-Performance Anode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Inorg. Nevertheless, the dissoln. of discharge products (Na2Se) and the structure collapse during Na+ insertion/extn. result in a dramatic decline of capacity.  Herein, SnSe nanosheets anchored on Ti3C2 (SnSe@Ti3C2) with a three-dimensional (3D) network structure is prepd. by electrostatic self-assembly and annealing strategy.  Ti3C2 with abundant functional groups and open pores facilitates the full reaction of the electrode and electrolyte.  In addn., the 3D network structure of Ti3C2 substantially reduces the structural collapse and aggregation of SnSe as well as improves charge transfer kinetics of the composites.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Achieving ultrastable cyclability and pseudocapacitive sodium storage in SnSe quantum-dots sheathed in nitrogen doped carbon nanofibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Wenxi; Ma, Xiaoqing; Li, Yadong; Wang, Guangzhao; Long, Xiaojiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Engineering suitable tin selenide (SnSe) anode for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) with both excellent cycling durability and remarkable rate capability has received enormous attention.  The effective regulation of morphol. and size has been shown to promote electrochem. performance.  The irregular shape and micron size of obtained SnSe by conventional construction methods (high energy ball milling) is detrimental to the anode performance.  Herein, SnSe quantum-dots (E-SnSe) and SnSe nanoparticles (B-SnSe) are synthesized by means of a feasible electrospinning technique together with subsequent high-temp. selenylation and high energy ball milling, resp., and both are examd. as anode materials for SIBs.  As compared to B-SnSe electrode, the E-SnSe electrode delivers a larger discharge capacity of 268 mAh g-1 after 750 cycles at 2 A g-1 and more remarkable cycling durability over 1500 cycles even at much higher rate of 5 A g-1, which is the best cycling stability reported so far for SnSe-based anodes.  The significantly enhanced performance is attributed to unique three-dimensional (3D) conductive carbon network structure of E-SnSe, which could boost rapid electron/sodium ion transport with the remarkable contribution of pseudocapacitance and dramatically improve the structure evolution of SnSe resulted from the conversion and alloying process.  Furthermore, the assembled Na3V2(PO4)3@C//E-SnSe full cell shows an output voltage of above 2.0 V, ultralong cycle life of 1500 cycles with a high reversible discharge capacity of about 100 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 and superior rate capability with 86.4% capacity retention after 35 cycles, showing the potential application prospects in a large scale energy storage field.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2022.232333"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Spatially Confined Construction of Heterostructured SnSe/SnTe Nanodots in Porous Carbon Fibers with High-Level N-Doping for Superior Sodium Storage</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Spatially confined construction of heterostructured SnSe/SnTe nanodots in porous carbon fibers with high-level N-doping for superior sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hua, Rong; Li, Hongbao; Zheng, Jun; Wang, Rui; Ma, Quanwei; Zhou, Tengfei; Zhang, Longhai; Kang, Hongwei; Zhang, Chaofeng; Zheng, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a promising candidate to supersede lithium ion batteries, however, the lack of suitable electrode materials for efficient Na-storage still hinders its practical use.  Hence, this work reports a general and effective way to in-situ construction of heterostructured SnSe/SnTe nanodots encapsulated in nitrogen-doped mesoporous carbon matrix (SnSe/SnTe@N-CNFs).  In such unique architecture, the porous N-CNFs matrix cannot only provide high electronic/ion cond. but also alleviate the vol. change of SnSe and SnTe, leading to rapid kinetics and robust structure, whereas the ultrasmall SnSe/SnTe heterostructure with built-in interfacial driving force can promote charge transfer kinetics.  Meanwhile, the abundant defective sites induced from high-level N-doping is beneficial to Na+ storage.  Besides, the formed Sn-C, Se-C, and Te-C bonds can improve the interfacial interaction between SnSe/SnTe and N-CNFs, thus enhancing electronic cond., suppressing the aggregation and detachment of active components, and ensuring rapid kinetics and structural integrity.  When evaluated as an anode for SIBs, the as-prepd. SnSe/SnTe@N-CNFs exhibits high reversible capacity, remarkable cycling stability, and superior rate capability.  This work can offer an efficient and universal strategy for designing advanced anode materials with exceptional performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c22817"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Spatially Confined Synthesis of SnSe Spheres Encapsulated in N, Se Dual-Doped Carbon Networks toward Fast and Durable Sodium Storage</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Unfortunately, the large vol. changes, low elec. cond., and shuttling effect of polyselenides have impeded their real application.  In this work, we present a spatially confined reaction approach for controllable fabrication of SnSe spheres, which are embedded in polydopamine (PDA)-derived N, Se dual-doped carbon networks (SnSe@NSC) through a one-step carbonization and selenization method. Consequently, the SnSe@NSC anode exhibits a high discharge capacity of 302.6 mA h g-1 over 500 cycles at 1 A g-1 and a competitive rate capability of 285.3 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1.  Addnl., a sodium ion full battery is assembled by coupling the SnSe@NSC anode with the cathode of Na3V2(PO4)3 and verified with good cycling durability (190 mA h g-1 at 1 A g-1 over 500 cycles) and high energy d. (204.3 W h kg-1).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently emerged as one of the favored contenders for use in medium and large-scale stationary energy storage owing to the abundance of the resources required to fabricate them, their low cost, and the fact that have properties similar to equiv. Li batteries.  However, their development also faces challenges such as poor cycling stability and unsatisfying rate performance.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">O3-type Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 cathode material for rechargeable sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jun; He, Xin; Zhou, Dong; Schappacher, Falko; Zhang, Xiaofei; Liu, Haidong; Stan, Marian Cristian; Cao, Xia; Kloepsch, Richard; Sofy, Mohamed Sayed; Schumacher, Gerhard; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3431-3437</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 cathode material for sodium-ion batteries has been synthesized by a solid-state reaction method.  The obtained Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 shows an O3-type structure, and delivers a discharge capacity of 117 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 10 mA g-1 in a range of 1.5-4.0 V at 20°.  Furthermore, the Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 cathode material shows good rate capability and cycling stability.  The working and structural transition mechanisms of the Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 material are examd. by ex-situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and in-situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) methods.  The valence state of Fe ions in the Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 material is estd. to be 2.67+.  The main redox couple is Ni2+/Ni4+, but the Fe2+/Fe3+ contributes a little as well at voltages below 2.0 V.  The original O3 phase transforms to a P3 phase during sodium extn. with good reversibility, but a slightly irreversible change of lattice parameters may lead to capacity decay during long-term cycling.  Moreover, the gas evolution during the first charge/discharge process is analyzed by using an operando mass spectrometry technique.  The obvious release of CO2 gas at the end of the charge process may be the other origin of the capacity decay.  Nevertheless, the absence of O2 evolution indicates an improved safety of the Na/Na[Fe1/3Ni1/3Ti1/3]O2 cell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.1c00938"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Kinetics-Driven One-Dimensional Growth of van der Waals Layered SnSe</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Kinetics-driven one-dimensional growth of van der Waals layered SnSe</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhibin; Ding, Keda; Liu, Zhifu; Zhang, Faqiang; Zeng, Huarong; Xu, Kunqi; Sun, Yiyang; Wang, Dong; Li, Yongxiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The one-dimensional (1D) form of van der Waals (vdW) layered compds. attracts growing interest due to their unusual phys. properties and potential for nanoelectronic devices.  However, the natural domain structure of vdW layered compds. is generally in a 2D form (nanosheets).  Here, we report the high-quality 1D growth of vdW layered SnSe nanoribbons (NRs) via vapor deposition.  The morphol. evolution of SnSe products was systematically investigated.  Angle-resolved polarized Raman spectroscopy and transmission electron microscopy analyses showed that the SnSe NRs are oriented along the [010] (zigzag, ZZ) direction.  The intact morphol. and good crystallinity suggest that the growth of SnSe NRs obeys a catalysis-free vapor-solid mechanism.  D. functional theory calcns. unveiled that the minimal migration barrier (0.33 eV) along the ZZ direction and the ZZ-oriented in-plane quasi-1D nature of SnSe are the main driving force of the 1D growth of vdW layered SnSe.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1007/s11581-019-03382-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Encapsulated SnSe in Carbon Nanofibers as Anode of Sodium Ion Batteries with Improved Properties</span>. <i>Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>26</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3937</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3946</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Encapsulated SnSe in carbon nanofibers as anode of sodium ion batteries with improved properties</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Xiaoqian; Ding, Shuangshuang; He, Hongcheng; Huang, Zhao; Cai, Mengqiu; Cai, Yong; Zhang, Ming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Ionics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3937-3946</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0947-7047</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnSe is commonly used as anode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high theor. sodium storage capacity (780 mAh/g).  However, low elec. cond. and serious vol. expansion during the processes of sodiation/desodiation lead to poor cyclic stability.  In this paper, a new synthesis strategy based on electrospinning was developed for encapsulated SnSe nanoparticles in carbon nanofibers (CNFs) to improve the structural and cyclic stability.  The significant feature of this method is that selenium (Se) powder is directly added into electrospinning soln. during annealing treatment to form SnSe nanoparticles encapsulated in CNFs instead of using Se powder during the thermal treatment process.  SnSe carbon nanofibers-1 (SnSe@CNFs-1) anode material exhibits the discharge capacities of 326 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g after 100 cycles and 249.4 mAh/g at 1 A/g after 900 cycles at room temp. (25°C).  It still exhibits stable electrochem. performance at low temp. (0°C) after 100 cycles of 267 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g.  This design not only improves the rate of electron transmission but also prevents SnSe nanoparticles from agglomerating and buffering the large vol. changes of electrode material during sodiation/desodiation processes.  This strategy also helps to alleviate pulverization of anode material during the recycling process and improve the cycle stability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2019.06.129"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sn-C Bonding Anchored SnSe Nanoparticles Grown on Carbon Nanotubes for High-Performance Lithium-Ion Battery Anodes</span>. <i>Appl.
